Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Transition-metal sulfides hold great promise as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries due to the high theoretical capacity and excellent redox reversibility based on multielectron conversion reactions. In this work, an elaborate composite, cobalt sulfide nanoclusters embedded in honeycomb-like sulfur-doped carbon foam (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF), is prepared via a facile sulfur-assisting calcination strategy, which tactfully induces the co-occurrence of in situ pore-forming, sulfidation, sulfur doping, and carbonization. Notably, sulfur-doped carbon foam (S-CF) possesses abundant voids, which are subject to construction of three-dimensional ionic/electronic pathways, leading to high sodium-ion accessibility and ultrafast sodium-ion/electron transportation toward Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters. When worked as an anode in sodium-ion batteries, it delivers a remarkable capacity of 373 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 1000 cycles at 0.25 C, achieving superior capacity retention of 80%. Furthermore, this anode could achieve unprecedented rate capability with a reversible capacity of 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C (20 A g<sup>–1</sup>), which significantly precedes those reported previously.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D19218%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYunxiao%2BWang%252C%2BYanxia%2BWang%252C%2BYun-xia%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D21%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b05134%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BFormation%2Bof%2BCo9S8%2BNanoclusters%2Bin%2BSulfur-Doped%2BCarbon%2BFoam%2Bas%2Ba%2BSustainable%2Band%2BHigh-Rate%2BSodium-Ion%2BAnode%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D19226%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290643&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Foams" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Foams" class="article__tags__link">Foams</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291353&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Rate capability" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Rate%20capability" class="article__tags__link">Rate capability</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cobalt+Sulfides+Nanoclusters&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cobalt%20sulfides%20nanoclusters">cobalt sulfides nanoclusters</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbon+Foam&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbon%20foam">carbon foam</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Co9s8+Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Co9S8%20anodes">Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrahigh+Rate+Capability&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrahigh%20rate%20capability">ultrahigh rate capability</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Energy storage technologies have attracted global attention over the past decades due to the ever-increasing demand for clean and efficient energy supply. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have become sophisticated rechargeable energy storage systems, which are extensively applied into portable electronic devices and hold great promise for powering electrical vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> However, Li is resource limited with uneven geographic distribution and the high cost of Li mineral impedes their practical applications in large-scale energy storage. In contrast, Na is abundant and cost-effective, mainly distributed in the ocean, and easy to obtain. Combined with a similar performance to their LIB counterparts, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are emerging as one of the potential candidates for stationary energy storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 2 to 5">(2−5)</a> Various low-cost anodes, including carbonaceous materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6−9)</a> phosphorus <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> and metal phosphides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> metals <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> and metal oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 2 and 18 to 20">(2,18−20)</a> and metal sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 21 to 28">(21−28)</a> have been intensively studied. Especially, metal sulfides based on multielectron conversion reactions, possessing high energy densities, and sustainable cyclablity have shown great potential for practical applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> has been paid close attention as an anode in NIBs due to its decent theoretical capacity (544 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), relatively low voltage plateau (0.3–0.7 V), and low cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a> Nevertheless, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode tends to show inferior reversibility, serious voltage hysteresis, and low cycling stability due to the intrinsic low conductivity and serious volume expansion during the sodiation/desodiation process. It is rational to engineer an ideal nanostructure with ultrafine active Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters embedded in high-conductivity carbon matrixes; especially, the porous C hosts can also serve as robust backbones to tolerate volume expansion of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. On the other hand, heteroatom doping, especially sulfur-doping, is believed to be effective to increase electronic conductivity and create defects and active sites in carbonaceous materials, which can accelerate Na-ion/electron transportation as well. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we develop a simple S-assisting calcination strategy, which achieves a unique composite with Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters confined in sulfur-doped carbon foam (denoted as Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF) for large-scale production. Benefiting from the ultrafine Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters and elaborate honeycomb-like structure of carbon foam, unique three-dimensional (3D) ionic and electronic pathways are constructed. When tested in NIBs, the as-obtained Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode demonstrates superior electrochemical properties for sodium-ion storage in terms of specific capacity, cycling stability, and rate capability: it exhibits a high initial reversible capacity of 467 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and possesses excellent reversible capacity retention of 80% (373 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) after prolonged 1000 cycles, which is 76.8 times higher than that of plain Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets (6.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Significantly, it delivers ultrahigh rate capability with a rate capability of 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C, suggesting its great promise for commercial utilization.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All of the chemicals were analytical grade and used without further purification. CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, sodium oleate, and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> were purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. Deionized water was used in all experiments.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Samples Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (0.316 g) was dissolved into 1.0 mL of H<sub>2</sub>O and mixed with 1.22 g of sodium oleate with continuous stirring for 3 h at 85 °C for further reaction. The obtained cobalt oleate precursor was homogeneously mixed with 10 g of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> and 0.5 g of sulfur by a planetary mixer. Subsequently, the produced complex was sintered at 600 °C with a heating rate of 3 °C min<sup>–1</sup> for 3 h in an Ar atmosphere. The calcinate was collected and immersed into 500 mL of H<sub>2</sub>O for 24 h. Afterwards, the mesoporous Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@C nanosheets were collected after washing the mixture with deionized water and ethanol three times, followed by drying at 80 °C in a vacuum oven overnight. For comparison, bare S-doped carbon foam (S-CF) was prepared from the mixture of sodium oleate, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, and S via the S-assisting calcination. Pure Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets were prepared according to a reported method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Structural Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies of the samples were investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, ULTRA/PLUS, ZEISS) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL, JEM-2010-fef). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method was utilized to calculate the specific surface areas (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>) through N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements (Micromeritics, ASAP 2020). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were collected by powder XRD (Bruker D8 ADVANCE) with Cu Kα radiation at a scan rate of 2° per minute; the ex situ XRD was performed at a scan rate of 0.5° per minute.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance was systematically evaluated using coin-type half-cells. The electrode slurry consisted of 80 wt % active materials (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF, S-CF, and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets), 10 wt % conductive carbon, and 10 wt % carboxymethyl cellulose binder. The mixed slurry was spread onto a Cu foil, followed by drying at 80 °C overnight under vacuum. The electrolyte used in this work was 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate/ethylene carbonate (1:1 in volume) and 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate additive (5 wt % FEC). A sodium disc was employed as both reference and counter electrodes with a glass fiber separator. The electrochemical performance was studied using a LAND Battery Tester during the cut-off voltage of 0.01–3 V. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were conducted by a CHI 660C electrochemical workstation (ChenHua Instruments Co., China).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, a facile S-assisting calcination method is developed for the synthesis of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF. First, cobalt oleate (Co(OL)<sub>2</sub>) was formed due to the self-assembly of oleate reverse micelles with Co ions, which possess a huge bulk structure as shown in Figure S1a,b (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Second, a homogenous precursor consisting of Co(OL)<sub>2</sub>, S, and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was prepared by strong mixing procedures using a planetary mixer. When heated in Ar atmosphere, the S sublimation, Co<sup>2+</sup> sulfidation, and OA<sup>–</sup> carbonization processes take place subsequently, which leads to the formation of the final product (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF). A product prepared from Co(OL)<sub>2</sub> and S but without Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> only showed loose and random particle structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c,d</a>), which indicates that the introduced Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> serves as a hard template to form flake-like structures. It is noticeable that the added sulfur plays multiple roles during these processes. Without S, the material obtained from Co(OL)<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> shows a solid nanosheet-like nanostructure, in which there are no voids on any nanoflake (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1e,f</a>). It can be speculated that S not only works as the raw material to react with Co<sup>2+</sup> for the formation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, but also leads to the unique foam-like texture of carbon backbones due to its rapid sublimation. In particular, it participates in the decomposition and carbonization of OA-species as well, resulting in favorable S-doping in carbon. As revealed via scanning electron microscope (SEM) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b) and TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), the sample possesses the evident honeycomb-like structure with abundant nanovoids. The low magnification SEM images of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF at different areas (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) confirm the high uniformity of this unique foam-like nanostructure. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d suggests the amorphous nature of carbon and low crystallization state of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. The lattice spacings are determined to be ∼2.85 and 2.3 Å, respectively, which correspond to the (222) and (331) planes of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> phase. As shown in the corresponding high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HADDF-STEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e), the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters are uniformly anchored in the C matrix with the nanosize of ∼2 nm. Meanwhile, the HADDF-STEM image with the energy-dispersive X-ray elemental mapping images of C, S, and Co (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) also further clearly demonstrates the unique foam-like nanostructure with elemental Co and S homogeneously distributed in CF backbones. The constructed nanoarchitecture is critical. The carbon matrix is favorable to enhance overall electrode conductivity and maintain the electrode integrity. The ultrasmall Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters surrounded by high-conductive C tend to show high Na-storage activity and alleviate the volume expansion during sodiation/desodiation processes. More importantly, the foam-like structure and S-doping of carbon foam are expected to cause lopsided charge distribution around sulfur vacancies, leading to accelerated electron transport and Na-ion diffusion. For comparison, pure Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets were prepared via the reported method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, the SEM image shows that small Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets are present on the surface; whereas, the TEM images indicate that the interior Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is seriously stacked together; the HRTEM image demonstrates that the lattice fringes with interplanar spacing is 3.03 Å, which could be indexed to be the (110) planes of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration for in situ formation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters confined in sulfur-doped carbon foam (denoted as Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF). (b) SEM image, (c) TEM image, (d) HRTEM with (e) corresponding HADDF-STEM images, and (f) elemental mapping of C (red), S (yellow), Co (green) of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The XRD patterns of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, indicate that both the samples could be indexed to Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (JCPDF no. 75-2236). The low intensity and broadened properties of the dominant diffraction peaks suggest the small nanosize and low crystallization of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> phase. In addition, a shoulder peak of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF is exhibited at about 26°, corresponding to amorphous carbon. The Raman spectra of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The Raman peaks at ∼ 474.8 and 679.1 cm<sup>–1</sup> are due to the existence of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Two prominent peaks at ∼1349.4 (D band) and 1601.1 cm<sup>–1</sup> (G band) related to carbon <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> are only observed in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF. The D band originates from the structural defects and disorder, whereas the G band corresponds to the first-order scattering of the E<sub>2g</sub> mode due to the sp<sup>2</sup> domains. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> It is noteworthy that it exhibits high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio (∼1.92, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), which indicates that there are many defects or disordered sites in the carbon in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF, corresponding to the amorphous feature of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The chemical states of the elements are investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c displays the C 1s spectrum fitted into three peaks at 284.3, 285.5, and 288.6 eV, which correspond to C–C/C═C, C–S, and O═C–O, respectively, indicating the S-heteroatom doping in C. Two peaks observed at 780.8 and 796.6 eV with two satellite peaks at 786.4 and 801.8 eV are ascribed to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). The high resolution XPS S 2p spectra shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e clearly shows two peaks at 160.0 and 164.1 eV, which are supposed to represent S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> bonding, respectively. An apparent peak at 168.3 eV is observed as well, which is attributed to a S shake up satellite peak and S–C bonding, further confirming that sulfur heteroatoms are incorporated into the carbon foam via S-assisting calcination in an Ar atmosphere. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> To evaluate the amount of C in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF, the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed from 50 to 900 °C at a rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> in air. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f, the weight loss in area I from 50 to 400 °C is likely due to the evaporation of water remaining in the sample and the decomposition of functional groups on the carbon foam; the evident weight loss (area II) between 400 and 500 °C is assigned to the combustion of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The oxidation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> takes place in the range of 500–900 °C. Assuming the final product is pure Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the weight loss during this process is ∼70.6%, corresponding to a Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> content of ∼32% in the composite. Similarly, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> ratio in the hybrid could be also evaluated to be ∼35% based on the weight loss (∼18.1%) of bare Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets during this temperature range (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns and (b) Raman spectra of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and pure Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets. High resolution XPS spectra of (c) Co 2p, (d) C 1s, and (e) S 2p of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF. (f) TGA curve of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF nanocomposite.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodiation/desodiation behaviors of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheet electrodes are evaluated by galvanostatic charge/discharge cycling. 2032 coin-type half-cells, paired with metallic Na foils, are used to investigate battery performance of these two anodes. CV curves of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF at a scan rate of 0.25 mV s<sup>–1</sup> during five cycles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. It shows one cathodic peak at around 1.0 V during the initial scan, which is attributed to the sodiation reaction of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> There is an obvious spike near 0 V, which is ascribed to Na-ion insertion into the interlayer of the graphitic nanocrystallites of the graphene nanosheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> During the subsequent cathodic cycles, evident peaks at around 0.8 V evolves and were repeated, which could be assigned to the reversible conversion reaction along with the formation of Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S. As for the anodic sweep, the obvious peak at ∼1.8 V corresponds to the desodiation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> It is noteworthy that the reduction peak at ∼0.8 V and the oxidation peak at ∼1.8 V is highly repeatable, suggesting high reversibility of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF during sodiation/desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Accordingly, the charge/discharge curves of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF battery at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> demonstrates that it presents a long slope without a clear plateau during the discharge process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), which is ascribed to the occurrence of various reactions, including sodiation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and rapid absorption of Na-ions into the S-doped carbon foams. However, It delivers a low initial Coulombic efficiency of 53.7%, which is due to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film and occurrence of irreversible side reactions. When further discharged to 0.01 V, metallic Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S can be formed. Meanwhile, the charge/discharge curves at selected cycles show sustainable performance and high reversible capacity during subsequent 1000 cycles, indicating the excellent Na-storage properties of the electrode. In contrast, a pair of cathodic and anodic peaks at around 0.65 and 1.74 V are observed for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode at the first cycle (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>), which is mainly corresponding to the conversion reaction between Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Na. However, the peak intensity is gradually decreased during the following cycles, indicating that Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets suffer from low capacity and poor cycling stability. Accordingly, the charge/discharge curves of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> show a long plateau at 0.75 V during the first discharge process, which delivers a discharge capacity of ∼810 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. However, it shows rapid capacity failure, retaining a low reversible capacity of ∼213 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after only 10 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Cyclic voltammograms of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode for the initial five cycles at a scan rate of 0.25 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Charge/discharge curves of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode for selected cycles. (c) Cycling performance of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets and S-CF electrodes at 0.25 C (1 C = 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (d) Rate capabilities at various current densities of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets and S-CF electrodes. (e) Comparison of rate capabilities of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF electrode with other anode materials of NIBs in the literature (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C sphere: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a>, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-PNS/GS: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a>, Co<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>S/FGN: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a>, CNT/CoS: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a>, Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>@polyaniline: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a>, and carbon-encapsulated CoS: ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the cycling behaviors of these two electrodes at 0.25 C over 1000 cycles are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. It is manifest that Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF outperforms Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets in terms of reversible capacity, capacity retention, and cycling stability. The Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode shows high reversible capacity retention of 80% (373 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) after 1000 cycles with Coulombic efficiency above 95%, which is very close to the theoretical capacity (390.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) of the hybrid, implying the nanoarchitectural stability of the unique foam-like structure. Moreover, the S-doping structure with enhanced conductivity is responsible for the accelerated charge-transfer rate and additional active sites, which endow the sample with high reaction activity and fast electron transport. In contrast, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets undergoes rapid capacity fade and become inactive with negligible reversible capacity retention of 8.3% (52 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) after 30 cycles. It is likely due to the intrinsic low conductivity and rapid collapse of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets structure without any framework protection during the large volume expansions of sodiation/desodiation processes. Impressively, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF exhibits superior overall performance in terms of cycling life (1000 cycles), reversible capacity (373 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and capacity retention (80%) over testing cycles (vs Co<sub><i>x</i></sub>S-based anodes, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Benefitting from the unique nanostructure, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF is expected to deliver outstanding rate capability. The rate capabilities of the two samples ranging from 0.25 to 50 C (1 C = 400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF delivers reversible capacities of 487, 387, 386, 340, 333, 280, 227, and 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.25, 0.5, 1.25, 2.5, 5, 12.5, 25, and 50 C, respectively. Significantly, when the current density is back to 1.25 C, the reversible capacity of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF could maintain 380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles. Furthermore, the corresponding charge/discharge curves of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>) exhibited an evident charge plateau even at 25 C with increased polarization over cycling. It is remarkable that the hybrid exhibits an unprecedented high-rate performance achieving ultrahigh rate capability of 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at ultrahigh 50 C. In sharp contrast, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets exhibit decent initial reversible capacity of 542 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, however, it rapidly declines to 260 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 10 cycles at 0.25 C; it becomes inactive with negligible capacity when the current rate is higher than 1.25 C. The results also indicate that the constructed bare Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets are easy to deteriorate during cycling. On the other hand, It is noteworthy that the S-CF component is responsible for the sustainability of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF composite. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c,d, bare S-CF delivers an average reversible capacity of ∼299 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to ∼200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the hybrid based on the C ratio. This result indicates that S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> equally contribute to the overall capacity of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF. The rate capability of S-CF is much inferior than the hybrid, which implies that the synergetic effects of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and S-CF components are responsible for the unprecedented rate capability of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF. In contrast with previous reports for NIBs, it shows superior rate capability (vs typical metal sulfide-based anodes, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e). The electrochemical performance of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF underscores that rational nanostructure is conclusive to its performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to investigate the high-rate kinetics of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF, CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 50 mV s<sup>–1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V are obtained, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a</a>. It is well known that the current intensity of a CV curve consists of surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled processes. The total capacity usually comprises the faradaic and nonfaradaic contributions, which could be calculated and are illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a (the detail of the calculation is shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). As displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9b</a>, the <i>b</i> value is calculated to be 0.7 (between 0.5 and 1) for the redox peaks, which demonstrates that electrochemical reactions of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF contain both faradaic and nonfaradaic processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> By calculating the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> values, the composition of capacitive effects and diffusion-controlled reactions could be distinguished. The capacitive contribution will dominate the reaction process when the surface capacitive contribution is higher than 50%. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, by the quantification, the contribution of surface capacitive capacity (red region) is as high as about 72% at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates that Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF possesses favorable charge-transfer kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The capacitive capacity increases with raising the scan rate and obtains a maximum value of 92% at 50 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). The high portion capacity from the surface-controlled process is favorable to realize high rate performance, which agrees with the outstanding rate capability in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. Ex situ XRD is conducted to identify the Na-storage mechanism during electrochemical measurement (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). The peaks of (311) and (222) could be detected in the fresh Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF electrode. When it was fully discharged to 0.01 V, the XRD signals of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF disappear, and two new peaks are developed, corresponding to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDF no. 47-0178) and Co (JCPDF no. 15-0806). When charged to 3 V, the peaks of (311) and (440) of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF could be recovered; meanwhile, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co decrease notably with possible Co residual. It confirms that the reaction between Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S is highly reversible, which is well consistent with stable cycling performance of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). This result suggests a Na-ion storage mechanism of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based nanocomposites via the multielectron conversion reaction (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> + 16Na ↔ 9Co + 8Na<sub>2</sub>S).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Capacitive (pink) and diffusion-controlled (purple) contribution to charge storage of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF at 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (Inset: normalized contribution ratio of capacitive (pink) and diffusion-controlled (purple) capacities at different scan rates of 0.1, 0.25, 0.5, 1, 2.5, 5, 10, 25, and 50 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively). (b) Ex situ XRD patterns of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode when discharged to 0.01 V and charged to 3 V. (c–e) SEM, TEM, and STEM images of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode after 1000 cycling, with the corresponding elemental mapping for Na (cyan), S (yellow), Co (red), C (green), and F (dark red).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The compositional and morphological changes in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets after 1000 cycles are investigated by SEM, TEM, and STEM in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c–e. As revealed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF retains the honeycomb-like nanostructure; whereas, the surface become rough due to the formation of thick SEI films. The elemental mappings of the electrode at fully discharged state after 1000 cycles show that Na and S elements have consistent signals, indicating the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S. The signals of Co and C element are observed around the Na<sub>2</sub>S phase, which is ascribed to the occurrence of reversible conversion reaction with the formation of Co nanograins. In addition, the corresponding phase mappings and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS)&nbsp;results further confirm the coexistence of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co phases (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). In addition, intensive F signal is collected as well, which is assigned to side-products with FEC additive and the formation of SEI film, which is homogenously dispersed in the sample. Interestingly, SEM images of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>) demonstrate that the morphology of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets have collapsed into dense and massive bulk after 1000 cycles, which verifies that the bare Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets cannot maintain its morphology during cycling and result in rapid capacity fading. The manifold functions of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF toward excellent Na-storage properties, therefore, could be speculated as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. First, the active Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> itself is ultrasmall nanoclusters and closely surrounded by high conductive C frameworks, which enable high reactivity in the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> component for complete sodiation/desodiation. Second, the open foam-like structure could expose more reaction sites and provide more ionic/electronic channels, leading to additional shortened route and fast mobility for 3D electronic and ionic transport. Third, the introduction of S-doping in the C foam could create a number of defects and active sites in C, which could increase the adsorbent ability for sodium ions, thereby enhancing surface capacitive effects. Moreover, the amorphous carbon matrix with abundant voids could not only enhance the overall conductivity and reaction rate, but also serves as the backbone to accommodate volume expansion and prevent the elaborate nanostructures from degradation.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/medium/am-2019-05134y_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Schematic illustration of the rational design of 3D ionic/electronic pathways of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF nanocomposite for high-performance Na-ion storage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/asset/images/large/am-2019-05134y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b05134&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the unique Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF structure has been successfully prepared by a facile sulfur-assisting calcination strategy. With thin carbon foam frameworks, S-doped abundant active sites/defects, and high reactive Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters for fast Na-ion storage, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode could deliver decent reversible capacity of 373 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over prolonged 1000 cycles and ultrahigh rate capability of 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 50 C. The developed sulfur-assisting calcination provides a new simple strategy to construct an elaborate Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode on a large-scale, which simultaneously achieves the formation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoclusters, elaborate carbon foam, and favorable S-doping, which significantly optimizes the Na-storage properties of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode and can be extended to develop various metal sulfide-based anodes for NIB applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b05134" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b05134</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">SEM images of three control samples, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF, and&nbsp;Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets; morphology of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanosheets; Raman spectra and Gaussian fitting of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF; TGA curve of the bare Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>; CV and charge/discharge curves of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> anode; galvanostatic charge and discharge curves of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF at various current rates; comparison of our Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF materials with previously published Co<sub><i>x</i></sub>S based anodes for&nbsp;sodium-ion batteries;&nbsp;CV curves of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF electrode at various scan rates; phase mapping and EDS results of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@S-CF anode after 1000 cycling; and SEM images, EDS spectrum, and elemental mappings of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>&nbsp;anode after 1000 cycling&nbsp;(<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b05134/suppl_file/am9b05134_si_001.pdf">am9b05134_si_001.pdf (1.77 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b05134" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the advent of flexible electronics, flexible lithium-ion batteries have attracted great attention as a promising power source in the emerging field of flexible and wearable electronic devices such as roll-up displays, touch screens, conformable active radio-frequency identification tags, wearable sensors and implantable medical devices.  In this review, we summarize the recent research progress of flexible lithium-ion batteries, with special emphasis on electrode material selectivity and battery structural design.  We begin with a brief introduction of flexible lithium-ion batteries and the current development of flexible solid-state electrolytes for applications in this field.  This is followed by a detailed overview of the recent progress on flexible electrode materials based on carbon nanotubes, graphene, carbon cloth, conductive paper (cellulose), textiles and some other low-dimensional nanostructured materials.  Then recently proposed prototypes of flexible cable/wire type, transparent and stretchable lithium-ion batteries are highlighted.  The latest advances in the exploration of other flexible battery systems such as lithium-sulfur, Zn-C (MnO2) and sodium-ion batteries, as well as related electrode materials are included.  Finally, the prospects and challenges toward the practical uses of flexible lithium-ion batteries in electronic devices are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpJ4RXf9cGm8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhQ9WOI-kyYqw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXltFWms7k%253D&amp;md5=3b202546afea097f62fc47655608ade6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzsShiqhEpPg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201200691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzsShiqhEpPg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-Structured NaTi2(PO4)3@C Nanocomposite as the Low Operation-Voltage Anode Material for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Dongxue; Liu, Qiang; Chen, Chaoji; Li, Malin; Meng, Xing; Bie, Xiaofei; Wei, Yingjin; Huang, Yunhui; Du, Fei; Wang, Chunzhong; Chen, Gang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">NASICON-type structured NaTi2(PO4)3 (NTP) has attracted wide attention as a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), whereas it still suffer from poor rate capability and cycle stability due to the low electronic cond.  Herein, the architecture, NTP nanoparticles embedded in the mesoporous carbon matrix, is designed and realized by a facile sol-gel method.  Different than the commonly employed potentials of 1.5-3.0 V, the Na+ storage performance is examd. at low operation voltages between 0.01 and 3.0 V.  The electrode demonstrates an improved capacity of 208 mAh g-1, one of the highest capacities in the state-of-the-art titanium-based anode materials.  Besides the high working plateau at 2.1 V, another one is obsd. at ∼0.4 V for the first time due to further redn. of Ti3+ to Ti2+.  Remarkably, the anode exhibits superior rate capability, whose capacity and corresponding capacity retention reach 56 mAh g-1 and 68%, resp., over 10,000 cycles under the high c.d. of 20 C rate (4 A g-1).  Worthy of note is that the electrode shows negligible capacity loss as the current densities increase from 50 to 100 C, which enables NTP@C nanocomposite as the prospective anode of SIBs with ultrahigh power d.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGou2hfV3h-MgbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPUVQwWJ-z6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtlSkug%253D%253D&amp;md5=0bafc7c1afc6de53c0aa0aa210d29351</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1039/c5ta05118e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile Synthesis of High Performance Hard Carbon anode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A highly reversible, resource-abundant and low-cost anode is indispensable to the future success of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) in large-scale energy storage application.  The authors report the facile synthesis of a biomass-derived hard carbon for SIBs by 1-step pyrolysis of shaddock peel under an inert atm. without activation or any further treatments.  The pyrolytic carbon shows very high reversible sodium storage capacities up to 430.5 mA h/g at a c.d. of 30 mA/g and superior cycling stability with only 2.5% capacity loss over 200 charge-discharge cycles.  The high capacity and excellent cycle performance, combined with the facile synthesis procedure make it a promising anode material for practical SIBs.  The good Na-ion storage property of the shaddock peel-derived pyrolytic carbon is attributed to its unique honeycomb-like morphol. with a shortened distance for Na-ion diffusion, and the large interlayer distance which is available for sodium insertion/extn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrUZb0nR_lduLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPUVQwWJ-z6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsVegtr7O&amp;md5=32487f69bba862d984793316e10ff8e4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2013.01.064"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reduced Graphene Oxide with Superior Cycling Stability and Rate Capability for Sodium Storage</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>57</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">202</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">208</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2013.01.064</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D57%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D202-208%26journal%3DCarbon%26author%3DY.-X.%2BWang%26author%3DS.-L.%2BChou%26author%3DH.-K.%2BLiu%26author%3DS.-X.%2BDou%26title%3DReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bwith%2BSuperior%2BCycling%2BStability%2Band%2BRate%2BCapability%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BStorage%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.carbon.2013.01.064&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b05134&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.carbon.2013.01.064&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reduced graphene oxide with superior cycling stability and rate capability for sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yun-Xiao; Chou, Shu-Lei; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">57</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">202-208</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising elec. energy storage systems for sustainable energy storage applications due to the abundance of Na and their low cost.  The electrochem. properties of Na ion storage in reduced graphene oxide (RGO) were studied in an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO4 in propylene carbonate (PC).  The RGO anode allowed significant Na ion insertion, leading to higher capacity at high c.d. compared to the previously reported results for C materials.  This is due to the fact that RGO possesses higher elec. cond. and is a more active host, with large interlayer distances and a disordered structure, enabling it to store more Na ions.  RGO anode exhibits high capacity combined with long-term cycling stability at high current densities, leading to reversible capacity ≤174.3 mA-h g-1 at 0.2 C (40 mA g-1), and even 93.3 mA-h g-1 at 1 C (200 mA g-1) after 250 cycles.  Also, RGO could yield a high capacity of 141 mA-h g-1 at 0.2 C (40 mA g-1) over 1000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr39AqQSVabh7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPUVQwWJ-z6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXjtVensLc%253D&amp;md5=704c607778e4fad1ece37d5d579584eb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b01419"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lotus Seedpod-Derived Hard Carbon with Hierarchical Porous Structure as Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">12554</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">12561</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lotus Seedpod-Derived Hard Carbon with Hierarchical Porous Structure as Stable Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Feng; Zhang, Minghao; Bai, Ying; Wang, Xinran; Dong, Ruiqi; Wu, Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">13</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12554-12561</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon material is one of the candidates with great promise as anode-active material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Here, new types of biomass-derived hard carbons were obtained via one-step carbonization of lotus seedpods at 1000-1400°, resp.  The control of carbonization temp. proved to be significant in controlling the lattice characterization of lotus seedpod-derived hard carbon.  Higher temp. generally promoted the lattice graphitization and thus generated a more narrowed d-interlayer space with limited pore vol.  The hard carbon pyrolyzed at 1200° achieved an optimized reversible capacity of 328.8 mAh g-1 and exhibited a remarkable capacity retention of 90% after 200 cycles.  In addn., such a biomass-derived hard carbon presented improved cyclic stability and rate performance, revealing capacity of 330.6, 288.9, 216.9, 116.5, and 78.3 mAh g-1 at 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000 mA g-1, resp.  Intrinsically, high pyrolysis temp. (1400°) gave rise to more narrowed carbon lattice and reduced pore vol. and, thus, failed to accommodate sodium ions either from the intercalation into lattice or the ion adsorption onto the pore surface.  Such combined advantages of lotus seedpod-derived hard carbon, including the abundance, sufficiently adequate reversible capacity, and prominent cycling and rate property allowed for its large-scale application as promising anode material for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpEusCsCPVmQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhPUVQwWJ-z6Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXltFCisb8%253D&amp;md5=c24e6a8c5e5878a4b5c35f70ef737928</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.8b01690"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Si, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fabrication of Microporous Sulfur-Doped Carbon Microtubes for High-Performance Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
